        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '64a4d823-1ac1-467c-8f9e-0c63fc064991'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d18288899" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Low-temperature synthesis of polyanionic cathodes for sodium-ion batteries is highly desirable but often plagued by prolonged reaction times and suboptimal crystallinity. To address these challenges, a novel self-adaptive coordination field regulation (SACFR) strategy based on a dynamic lock-and-release (DLR) mechanism is introduced. Specifically, urea is used as a DLR carrier during synthesis, which dynamically “locks” and “releases” vanadium ions for controlled release, simultaneously “locking” H<sup>+</sup> ions to enhance phosphate group release, thereby creating a self-adaptive coordination field that can intelligently respond to real-time demands of the reaction system. This dynamic coordination behavior contributes to both an improvement in reaction kinetics and a significant reduction in Gibbs free energy change (<i>ΔG</i>). As a result, the kinetic efficiency and thermodynamic spontaneity of the reaction are greatly enhanced, enabling the efficient synthesis of high-crystalline Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F (NVOPF) at 90 °C within just 3 hours. The as-prepared NVOPF cathode exhibits exceptional rate performance and ultra-stable cycling stability across a broad temperature range. Furthermore, the successful kilogram-scale synthesis underscores the practical potential of the innovative strategy. This work pioneers the regulation of coordination field chemistry for polyanionic cathode synthesis, providing transformative insights into material design.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202413013-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202413013-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have garnered significant attention as a sustainable technology for large-scale energy storage and smart electric grids, owing to the abundance and cost-effectiveness of sodium.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0001_R_d18288762e447" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Chem. Soc. Rev. Adv. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The performance of high-quality SIBs largely depends on advanced electrode materials, among which cathode material plays a pivotal role in determining energy density, cycling life, and overall battery performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0002_R_d18288762e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater. Energy Mater. Adv. Adv. Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thereinto, the sodium vanadium fluorophosphate series, known as Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2−2</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>1+2</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> (0&nbsp;≤&nbsp;<i>x&nbsp;</i>≤&nbsp;1, NVPFs; <i>x</i> = 0, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F (NVOPF)), have attracted considerable research interest due to their impressive theoretical specific capacity (128–130 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and wide operating voltage range (3.6–4.1&nbsp;V).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0003_R_d18288762e492" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Energy Lett. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the large-scale production of NVPFs faces obstacles due to the high costs associated with existing synthesis methods, including high-temperature solid-state methods, sol-gel methods, and solvent-thermal methods. It is noteworthy that processes involving high-temperature sintering should lead to the following four serious problems: 1) VF<sub>3</sub> volatilization leads to impurities and compromised electrochemical performance;<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0004_R_d18288762e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nano Energy">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 2) increased synthesis temperatures result in significant energy consumption and higher production costs; 3) irregular morphology of synthesized products hampers the achievement of high energy density in SIBs; 4) HF generation at high temperatures corrodes equipment, further increasing manufacturing costs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0003_R_d18288762e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Energy Lett. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, there is an urgent need to explore low-temperature and high-efficiency synthesis methods.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Recent attempts to synthesize NVOPF at low-temperature have shown promise, but there are still significant limitations, such as prolonged reaction times and suboptimal crystallinity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0003_R_d18288762e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Energy Lett. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv.">3</a>, <a href="#adma202413013-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0005_R_d18288762e513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Joule">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We found that these methods are primarily designed based on chemical reaction equations (Equation (1)) by adding specific agents such as NH<sub>4</sub>OH and NaOH to regulate reaction equilibrium, thus facilitating the formation of NVOPF. However, the fundamental nature of chemical equilibrium has been completely overlooked.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0006_R_d18288762e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Considering the principles of classical nucleation theory and crystal growth kinetics, it is well known that both nucleation (<i>N</i>) and growth rates (<i>G</i>) involved in product formation are directly dependent on supersaturation (<i>S</i>), the key parameter that significantly impacts product crystallinity during crystallization (Equations (2) and (3)).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0007_R_d18288762e532" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Namely, precisely controlling <i>S</i> in the reaction process is crucial for effective nucleation and growth of the target material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0008_R_d18288762e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As indicated in Equation 4, which elucidates the relationship between <i>S</i> and the concentration of reactant ions, <i>C</i><sub>M</sub> represents the concentration of metal cations (Na<sup>+</sup> and VO<sup>2+</sup>), <i>C</i><sub>DA</sub> represents the concentration of dissociated reactant anions (PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> and F<sup>−</sup>), <i>δ</i> denotes the number of ions in the material's formula unit, and <i>K</i><sub>sp</sub> stands for the solubility product constant.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0009_R_d18288762e569" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Chem.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In low-temperature synthesis, which typically involves solution chemistry systems, Na<sup>+</sup> and F<sup>−</sup> exist as free ions and can quickly respond to reaction changes. In contrast, the concentrations of VO<sup>2+</sup> and PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> are greatly regulated by their coordination environment, showing a certain degree of latency in response to reaction changes. The regulation of the coordination field directly affects the behavior of these ions, thereby indirectly regulating the supersaturation during the reaction. Thus, the key to achieving the low-temperature and efficient synthesis of high-crystallinity NVOPF materials lies in precisely controlling the coordination field of VO<sup>2+</sup> and PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> ions.

         </div>
         
         <p>Given this, re-examining traditional high-temperature preparation methods for NVOPF, citric acid (CA) or oxalic acid (OA) are commonly used as both reducing agents and carbon sources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0003_R_d18288762e794" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Energy Lett. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv.">3</a>, <a href="#adma202413013-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0004_R_d18288762e794" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nano Energy">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, these strong chelating agents form stable multi-dentate complexes with vanadium ions through multiple coordination sites, representing a form of static and strong coordination field regulation. The fully locked VO<sup>2+</sup> ions cannot adaptively release, making it impossible to dynamically respond to changes in reaction conditions. Additionally, the acidity of CA or OA can also interfere with the coordination environment of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup>, preventing the dynamical change of the coordination field around PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> species in reaction conditions. These dual negative impacts on the coordination fields of VO<sup>2+</sup> and PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> render low-temperature synthesis unfeasible, necessitating reliance on high temperatures and pressures to provide the additional driving force for nucleation and growth. This raises the critical question: if we can achieve dynamic regulation of the coordination fields of these key ions VO<sup>2+</sup> and PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup>, can we overcome the limitations of high-temperature methods, and realize efficient low-temperature synthesis of NVOPF?</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we introduce a novel self-adaptive coordination field regulation (SACFR) strategy based on the dynamic lock-and-release (DLR) mechanism. Using urea as a DLR agent, this approach enables the efficient production of high-crystalline NVOPF cathode material at a sub-boiling temperature of 90 °C within 3 hours. Importantly, we unveil the critical role of coordination field regulation in solution chemistry, previously overlooked in fluorophosphate preparation. Unlike traditional agents such as strong chelating agents (e.g., CA) and strong bases (e.g., NaOH), which exhibit static and strong coordination field regulation. Specifically, CA forms stable multi-dentate complexes that inhibit VO<sup>2+</sup> release, preventing the formation of NVOPF (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0001">1a</a>); and NaOH's strong ionic bonds rapidly bind with H<sup>+</sup>, leading to an avalanche-like release of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup>, ultimately resulting in uncontrolled crystal nucleation and growth (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0001">1b</a>), thereby limiting the flexibility of the reaction environment. Interestingly, urea can act as a dynamic regulator by “locking” and “releasing” VO<sup>2+</sup> ions through its carbonyl oxygen (C = O) to ensure their controlled release, while simultaneously “locking” H<sup>+</sup> ions via its −NH<sub>2</sub> groups to facilitate the “release” of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> ions, allowing real-time adaptation to reaction changes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0001">1c</a>). This creates a self-adaptive coordination field that maintains reactant concentrations conducive to reaction kinetics, promoting efficient crystal nucleation and growth. Moreover, our first-time determination of the <i>K</i><sub>sp</sub> of NVOPF (7.48×10<sup>−37</sup> at 363 K) provides an accurate assessment of solution supersaturation during NVOPF preparation and lays a fundamental basis for understanding its nucleation and growth mechanisms. Further calculations reveal that the addition of urea significantly reduces the Gibbs free energy change (<i>ΔG</i>), thus enhancing the spontaneity of the reaction. The dual advantages in kinetics and thermodynamics enable the efficient formation of NVOPF at lower temperatures while ensuring uniform and stable crystal growth. As a result, the as-prepared NVOPF material demonstrates exceptional rate performance and remarkable cycling stability across a broad temperature spectrum, including room temperature and extreme conditions (−20 °C/55 °C), underscoring its suitability for all-climate applications. Furthermore, the successful large-scale synthesis of NVOPF highlights the feasibility and potential of this synthesis strategy, paving the way for practical applications of high-performance polyanionic cathodes in sodium-ion batteries.</p>

            <figure class="figure" id="adma202413013-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/62a1b911-7601-461b-889a-5434ed7eb503/adma202413013-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>

               </figcaption>
