         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202413013-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202413013-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202413013-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202413013-sec-0030-title">2.1 Dynamic Lock-And-Release Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>Achieving high crystallinity in NVOPF necessitates precise regulation of the coordination field of VO<sup>2+</sup> and PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> ions. In aqueous systems, VO<sup>2+</sup> ions are generated from the thorough dissociation of VOSO<sub>4</sub> (VOS), which can be prepared from waste vanadium slag acid leach solution via solvent extraction, thereby reducing material costs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). These VO<sup>2+</sup> ions then interact with H<sub>2</sub>O molecules or coordination agents like CA and urea, forming solvated VO<sup>2+</sup> ions. As demonstrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information), CA prevents product formation, while urea facilitates high-yield production of NVOPF. To understand how different coordination agents influence the behavior of VO<sup>2+</sup> ions, it is essential to compare the binding strengths of these agents with VO<sup>2+</sup>. The specific forms of CA present at the experimental pH were also considered to ensure an accurate representation of the solvation structure under realistic experimental conditions (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Generally, solvated ion solutions coordinated with different ligands exhibit different colors. The stronger the field strength of the ligand, the absorption bands of <i>d-d</i> transitions move in the short wavelength region, deepening the color of the solvated ions solution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0010_R_d18288762e924" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0002">2a</a> shows digital photographs of VOS solutions with different agents, with colors gradually intensifying from left to right, and the color change becomes more obvious after a week, indicating the enhanced coordination ability of the three molecules toward VO<sup>2+</sup> (CA &gt;urea &gt;H<sub>2</sub>O). To evaluate the coordination ability of VO<sup>2+</sup> with the three molecules, we calculated the binding energy (BE) using density functional theory (DFT) with consideration for solvation effects, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0002">2b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0011_R_d18288762e949" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Storage Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The VO<sup>2+</sup>-CA pair exhibits the highest BE of −4.5&nbsp;eV, while the VO<sup>2+</sup>-urea pair has a lower BE of −3.0&nbsp;eV. The strong VO<sup>2+</sup>-CA interaction forms a more constraining coordination field, hindering the release of VO<sup>2+</sup> into the solution. Then, electrostatic potential (ESP) mapping was carried out to investigate the charge distribution of different solvated VO<sup>2+</sup> models. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0002">2c</a> shows a slight decrease in the ESP value after the introduction of urea into the original primary solvation shell (PSS) of VO<sup>2+</sup>. However, the incorporation of CA results in a pronounced reduction, indicating a stronger coordination field. Molecular dynamics (MD) simulations were further performed to elucidate the internal mechanisms of coordination agents in modulating the VO<sup>2+</sup> solvation structure. The statistical results in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information) show that the PSS of VO<sup>2+</sup> in VOS system consists of five H<sub>2</sub>O molecules. In contrast, new solvation structures with four H<sub>2</sub>O molecules and one CA or urea were observed in the VOS-CA and VOS-urea systems, demonstrating their effectiveness in reshaping the coordination field of VO<sup>2+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0002">2d,f</a>). Additionally, the contribution of SO<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup> to the VO<sup>2+</sup> solvation sheath has been fully considered, ensuring the robustness and accuracy of the model (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). Corresponding radial distribution functions (RDFs) and coordination number (CN) analysis in these three solution systems were then obtained to investigate the solvation sheath structure of VO<sup>2+</sup>. For pure VOS system, a sharp peak of VO–O (H<sub>2</sub>O) at ∼2.1 Å indicates a structured arrangement of water molecules around the VO<sup>2+</sup> solvation shell (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). For the VOS-CA and VOS-urea systems, new peaks corresponding to VO–O (CA) and VO–O (urea) appeared at ∼2.0 Å and ∼2.1 Å, respectively, indicating a stronger propensity for CA than urea to enter the PSS of VO<sup>2+</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0002">2e,g</a>). Besides, the VO–O (CA) peak is sharper than the VO–O (urea) peak, with a CN of 0.46, indicating a more constricting coordination field between CA and VO<sup>2+</sup> compared to urea and VO<sup>2+</sup>, consistent with the DFT results. It is indicated that urea, with its moderate binding energy, could tailor the coordination field chemistry, ensuring the controlled release of VO<sup>2+</sup> ions through its dynamic coordination ability.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202413013-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/43e768ef-8df0-4e1c-9963-29b91a7c54c3/adma202413013-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/43e768ef-8df0-4e1c-9963-29b91a7c54c3/adma202413013-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/70d37931-e279-413a-aa18-443b81b7f3bd/adma202413013-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/43e768ef-8df0-4e1c-9963-29b91a7c54c3/adma202413013-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202413013-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202413013" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Digital photograph of different VOS solution systems. b) Binding energies between VO<sup>2+</sup> and different molecules (H<sub>2</sub>O, urea, and CA). c) ESP mapping of the original VO<sup>2+</sup>-5(H<sub>2</sub>O), urea-VO<sup>2+</sup>-4(H<sub>2</sub>O) and CA-VO<sup>2+</sup>-4(H<sub>2</sub>O) solvation structures. d) 3D snapshots and partial enlarged snapshot representing VO<sup>2+</sup> solvation structure obtained from MD simulations and e) calculated RDFs and CN of VOS-CA system. f) 3D snapshots and partial enlarged snapshot representing VO<sup>2+</sup> solvation structure obtained from MD simulations and g) Calculated RDFs and CN of VOS-urea system.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To further demonstrate why urea is preferred as a dynamic coordination agent to effectively control VO<sup>2+</sup> release, a series of analytical techniques were conducted, including nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR), Fourier-transform infrared (FT-IR) spectroscopy, Raman spectroscopy, UV–visible spectrophotometry (UV-vis) and synchrotron-based X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) measurements, encompassing both X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) and extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS). <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0003">3a</a> shows that the <sup>2</sup>H peak shifting from 4.698 to 4.703&nbsp;ppm upon introducing VOS, indicating decreased electronic density and weakened proton shielding in water molecules.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0012_R_d18288762e1071" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The introduction of urea shifts the peak further, and even more shifting is observed with CA, indicating weakened solvation interactions and more free water release.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0013_R_d18288762e1077" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This illustrates the strong static coordination effect of CA on the solvation structure of VO<sup>2+</sup>, contrasting with the dynamic coordination provided by urea. FTIR spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0003">3b</a>) reveal a gradual blueshift of the SO<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup> vibration stretching peak with the addition of urea and CA, implying gradual degradation of electrostatic interaction between VO<sup>2+</sup> and SO<sub>4</sub><sup>2−</sup>, further confirming the regulated VO<sup>2+</sup> solvation structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0014_R_d18288762e1098" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Simultaneously, the stretching vibration signal of the O−H bond of solvated H<sub>2</sub>O shifts from 3293.9 to 3283.6 cm<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information), indicating penetration of the inner solvation sheath of VO<sup>2+</sup> and release of bound H<sub>2</sub>O into the bulk solution phase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0015_R_d18288762e1116" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0003">3c</a> shows a gradual blueshift and significant suppression of the O−H stretching vibration at 3050−3500 cm<sup>−1</sup>, indicating disruption of the original hydrogen bonding network in water molecules and a reduction in water activity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0016_R_d18288762e1126" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Environ. Sci.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Complexes of transition metal ions exhibit specific absorption peaks or bands in UV-vis spectra. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0003">3d</a> illustrates two characteristic absorption peaks of tetravalent vanadium in VOS, with a shoulder peak at 631&nbsp;nm and a prominent maximum absorption peak at 762&nbsp;nm.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0017_R_d18288762e1135" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Commun.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Introduction of urea and CA intensifies the absorption peak signal at 394&nbsp;nm (corresponding to the absorption peak of V<sup>3+</sup>). The apparent oxidation states of vanadium ions in the VOS, VOS-urea and VOS-CA systems are determined to be 3.962, 3.631 and 3.434, respectively, using <i>Lambert-Beer</i>’s law.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0018_R_d18288762e1145" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Int. J. Mol. Sci.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These thorough analyses indicate that urea provides a dynamic coordination field compared to CA, which forms a rigid and static coordination environment, thereby hindering the release of VO<sup>2+</sup> ions. In contrast, urea allows for a controlled and efficient release of VO<sup>2+</sup> ions.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202413013-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/99962de0-86f0-4cf0-92bc-931677911669/adma202413013-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/99962de0-86f0-4cf0-92bc-931677911669/adma202413013-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b46203ee-95c7-44d4-b5bc-3cb7d909838a/adma202413013-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/99962de0-86f0-4cf0-92bc-931677911669/adma202413013-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202413013-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202413013" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Spectral characterization of different VOS solution systems. a) NMR, b) FTIR, c) Raman spectra, and d) UV–vis spectra. e) Normalized V K-edge XANES and f) FT-EXAFS spectra in <i>R</i> space. For comparison, data for V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, VO<sub>2</sub>, and V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> powders are included. The <i>k</i><sup>3</sup>-weighted WT-EXAFS contour plots for g) VOS, h) VOS-urea, and i) VOS-CA solution systems. V K-edge EXAFS fitting curves in <i>k</i> space for j) VOS, k) VOS-urea, and l) VOS-CA solution systems.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The synchrotron-based XAS analysis, which offers higher precision and sensitivity to the subtle evolution of ionic coordination environments compared to conventional techniques, was employed to further investigate the coordination dynamics of VO<sup>2+</sup> ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0019_R_d18288762e1197" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The V K-edge XANES spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0003">3e</a>) for VOS, VOS-urea, and VOS-CA show that the absorption-edge energy shifts to a lower position with urea, and even more significantly with CA, indicating a reduction in vanadium oxidation state and alteration of the local coordination environment of VO<sup>2+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0020_R_d18288762e1208" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. eScience">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Fourier-transformed (FT) V K-edge EXAFS spectra of R-space (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0003">3f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information), which are more sensitive to local chemical variations, reveal an increase in V−O bond length with the introduction of urea into VOS, and an even greater increase with CA (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). This increase suggests stronger interactions between VO<sup>2+</sup> and the coordination agents. Additionally, the second-shell (V-O-V) scattering intensity decreases with urea and more significantly with CA, reflecting an electron transition to the 3<i>d<sub>xy</sub></i> state of the V <i>t<sub>2</sub></i>&nbsp;<i><sub>g</sub></i> orbit.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0021_R_d18288762e1236" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This reduced intensity indicates a more disrupted local coordination environment with CA, enhancing vanadium complex stability but hindering VO<sup>2+</sup> ion release. In contrast, urea maintains an adaptive coordination environment, beneficial for dynamic processes and optimal for NVOPF crystal nucleation and growth by allowing VO<sup>2+</sup> ions to be released as needed. <i>k</i><sup>3</sup>-weighted wavelet transforms (WT) of the V K-edge EXAFS data (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0003">3g−i</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information) reveal progressive shifts in the intensity maximum for the V−O bond, moving from 6.60 Å<sup>−1</sup> in VOS to 6.74 Å<sup>−1</sup> in VOS-urea, and 7.12 Å<sup>−1</sup> in VOS-CA. These shifts highlight the formation of distinct V−O coordination environments: a stronger, static environment with CA, and an adaptive, dynamic one with urea, consistent with the DLR mechanism. Quantitative least-squares fittings of the EXAFS curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0003">3j–l</a>) show that the average V−O coordination number in the first shell for VOS-CA is 5.1, lower than for VOS-urea (5.7) and VOS (5.8). The reduced coordination number with CA suggests more stable complexes, aligning with its static coordination effect. Conversely, urea's moderate coordination number supports its role in adaptive coordination, crucial for maintaining a dynamic environment. This adaptability ensures VO<sup>2+</sup> ions are released when needed and stabilized when not, providing optimal conditions for high-quality NVOPF crystal nucleation and growth. These comprehensive analyses underscore urea's superior performance as an adaptive and dynamic coordination agent, effectively regulating VO<sup>2+</sup> release and ensuring optimal conditions for target material synthesis.</p>
            
            <p>Another key reactant ion that affects supersaturation is the PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> concentration, primarily determined by the choice of phosphorus source, typically H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>HPO<sub>4</sub>, or Na<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>. Phosphoric acid ionizes in a stepwise manner (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0004">4a</a>), effectively acting as an intrinsic DLR mechanism, ensuring a controlled and gradual release of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> ions. However, Na<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> releases PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> too rapidly, overwhelming the system and resulting in uncontrolled nucleation and growth, which leads to rapid supersaturation and poor crystallinity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>’s progressive intrinsic DLR action results in insufficient PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> for effective nucleation, as the slow release fails to maintain the necessary supersaturation levels for crystal growth. NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>HPO<sub>4</sub>, therefore, are preferred as they offer a balanced ionization rate that supports steady supersaturation conducive to NVOPF nucleation and growth, aligning with the principles of the intrinsic DLR mechanism, which seeks to maintain an optimal reaction environment through controlled lock and release of ions. However, relying solely on these sources does not consistently yield high-crystalline NVOPF due to still inadequate PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> concentration and supersaturation for optimal conditions (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), highlighting the limitations of the intrinsic DLR mechanism.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202413013-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6093049a-a6a5-467b-98d8-1cde738ba6a2/adma202413013-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6093049a-a6a5-467b-98d8-1cde738ba6a2/adma202413013-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c9b0a011-0051-4cdc-9633-6946b0ef462b/adma202413013-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6093049a-a6a5-467b-98d8-1cde738ba6a2/adma202413013-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202413013-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202413013" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Stepwise ionization processes of phosphorus sources and the ionization constants of each step. b) Different agents and their associated α and S conditions relevant to the system, along with c) the XRD patterns of the resulting NVOPF products (NVOPF-OH and NVOPF-U refer to products obtained with NaOH and urea, respectively). d) pH variations in the reaction solution over time. e) XRD patterns of products prepared with three different phosphorus sources. f) Variation in V ion concentration. g) Comparison of <i>ΔG</i> for reactions with and without urea. h) Schematic illustration of our SACFR strategy, underpinned by the DLR mechanism, validated using urea as a DLR carrier. i) Comparison of energy consumption and processing time between our strategy and previously reported methods in the literature.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To further enhance PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> concentration, external regulation is required to expedite the ionization-to-PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> reaction, the rate-determining step. According to Le Chatelier's principle, reducing H<sup>+</sup> concentration is an effective strategy. A commonly adopted approach is to use strong bases like NaOH, which quickly dissociates in solution, releasing a significant quantity of OH<sup>−</sup> ions. These hydroxide ions catalyze ionization-to-PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> reaction, leading to a sudden increase in PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> concentration and inducing high supersaturation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0004">4b</a>; Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The first-time determination of NVOPF's <i>K</i><sub>sp</sub> (7.33&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−37</sup> at 298 K and 7.48&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−37</sup> at 363 K) using the conductivity method marks a critical step in understanding NVOPF's solubility and precipitation behavior. This data is essential for refining the DLR-driven strategy to optimize supersaturation control and improve crystallinity. NaOH's strong ionic bonds exhibit static regulation capabilities, leading to uncontrolled crystal nucleation and growth (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0004">4c</a>). As previously mentioned, while NH<sub>4</sub>OH is generally considered for modulating the acid-base environment,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0003_R_d18288762e1401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Energy Lett. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv.">3</a>, <a href="#adma202413013-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0004_R_d18288762e1401" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nano Energy">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> its application has several limitations. Its relatively narrow control range and inherent volatility cannot ensure accurate pH regulation throughout the reaction. Such fluctuations in H<sup>+</sup> concentration may compromise the homogeneity, stability, and reproducibility of the synthesized materials. In contrast, urea, serving as a self-adaptive coordination agent, plays a crucial role in regulating the “release” of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> ions by dynamically “locking” H<sup>+</sup> ions through its −NH<sub>2</sub> groups. The nitrogen atoms in these groups, with their lone electron pairs, exhibit a strong affinity for H<sup>+</sup> ions due to sp<sup>2</sup> hybridization and <i>p</i>, <i>π</i>-conjugation effects.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0022_R_d18288762e1425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Chem. Rev.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To verify urea's role, we monitored pH changes, observing a steady rise after urea introduction, stabilizing at 6.1 after 2.5 hours (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0004">4d</a>). This pH increase indicates a reduction in H<sup>+</sup> concentration, confirming urea's effectiveness in “locking” H<sup>+</sup> ions and facilitating controlled PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> release. The −NH<sub>2</sub> group of urea could also contribute to the observed pH variations via hydrolysis, though its impact is likely negligible at sub-boiling temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0023_R_d18288762e1443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Ind. Eng. Chem. Res.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The stabilization of pH corresponds with substantial light green precipitate formation, visually confirming the reaction's completion (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0004">4d</a> inset). Urea optimizes the “lock” and “release” of key reactant ions, maintaining optimal reactant concentrations and ensuring favorable reaction kinetics. Consequently, NVOPF-1Na and NVOPF-2Na (derived from NaH<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>HPO<sub>4</sub>, respectively) exhibit high crystallinity of pure-phase NVOPF, with NVOPF-1Na showing higher crystallinity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0004">4e</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13, S14</a>, and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast, NVOPF-3Na (from Na<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>) has lower crystallinity and impurities, consistent with the earlier discussion.</p>
            
            <p>To demonstrate the extent of the reaction and systematically study urea's impact on VO<sup>2+</sup> coordination field, we used inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES) to measure VO<sup>2+</sup> concentration variations (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0004">4f</a>). Initially, VO<sup>2+</sup> concentration remains unchanged during the first 60 minutes, indicating the initial dynamic “lock” phase, where urea forms temporary complexes with vanadium ions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0004">4f</a> inset shows the solution gradually becoming cloudy, corroborating this observation). From 60 to 120 minutes, the VO<sup>2+</sup> concentration rapidly declines, corresponding to the “release” mechanism, where temporary urea-VO<sup>2+</sup> complexes release VO<sup>2+</sup> ions, synchronizing with NVOPF crystal formation. This temporal and spatial separation between nucleation and growth phases offers significant advantages, including improved crystallinity, faster crystallization rates, and reduced defects, enabling precise control of stage conditions and laying a foundation for efficient, well-crystalline NVOPF production.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0024_R_d18288762e1496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Science">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Integrating principles from coordination chemistry, thermodynamics, and reaction kinetics, we designed a dynamic system that adjusts to real-time changes in the coordination environment. This innovative approach contrasts sharply with the more constraining coordination field imposed by CA and the static regulation induced by NaOH. Urea stands out due to its ability to modulate the coordination environment dynamically, enabling a finely tuned balance between the coordination and release of key reactant ions. The unique molecular structure of urea enables it to act as a dynamic regulator in the lock-and-release mechanism of key reactant ions. Specifically, the carbonyl oxygen (C = O) of urea forms a moderate coordination with VO<sup>2+</sup> ions, facilitating their release in a well-controlled manner, while the −NH<sub>2</sub> groups dynamically “lock” H<sup>+</sup> ions, promoting the timely “release” of PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> ions. This dual coordination function allows urea to adjust the coordination environment dynamically in response to the real-time demands of the reaction system. As a result, a self-adaptive coordination field is established, ensuring optimal reactant concentrations and favorable kinetics are maintained throughout the entire reaction process. To quantify urea's impact on reaction energetics, we further calculated the <i>ΔG</i> value for the studied reaction (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The results clearly show that the addition of urea significantly reduces <i>ΔG</i> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0004">4g</a>), making the reaction thermodynamically more favorable and increasing its spontaneity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0025_R_d18288762e1525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Commun.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The dual favorability in both kinetics and thermodynamics enables the efficient formation of high-crystalline NVOPF within just 3 hours at 90 °C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0004">4h</a>). This represents a significant advancement over traditional methods (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0004">4i</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), offering the most energy- and time-efficient approach for NVOPF synthesis to date, making it highly suitable for large-scale industrial production of polyanionic cathode materials.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202413013-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202413013-sec-0040-title">2.2 Structure and Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>The XRD pattern and refined results for the synthesized NVOPF are depicted in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0005">5a</a>. All diffraction peaks are well indexed to the NASICON-structured tetragonal crystal with an <i>I</i>4/<i>mmm</i> space group, and the low R-factor values (R<i>wp</i> = 7.66%, R<i>p</i> = 9.95%) verifies the reliable structural analyses.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0017_R_d18288762e1564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Commun.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> No traces of impurities are detected, indicating the high purity of the product. Detailed crystallographic and Rietveld refinement data for NVOPF are available in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information). The unit cell parameters (<i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 6.3760 Å and <i>c</i> = 10.6548 Å) closely match previously reported values.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0005_R_d18288762e1582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Joule">5</a>, <a href="#adma202413013-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0017_R_d18288762e1582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Commun.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The 2D grazing-incidence X-ray diffraction (GIXRD) pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0005">5b</a>) and its radially integrated intensity profile (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0005">5c</a>) exhibit distinct (002), (200), and (202) peaks, further confirming the high crystallinity of the as-synthesized NVOPF. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information) illustrates the schematic diagram of the tetragonal structure of NVOPF, characterized by [V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>10</sub>F] dioctahedra and [PO<sub>4</sub>] tetrahedra sharing oxygen atoms, forming channels along <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> axes, which serve as Na<sup>+</sup> ion pathways at the tunnel sites. FTIR spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information) further elucidate the molecular structure, closely corresponding to reported NVOPF spectra.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0003_R_d18288762e1613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Energy Lett. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv.">3</a>, <a href="#adma202413013-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0026_R_d18288762e1613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Typical features include a broad band (1000−1050 cm<sup>−1</sup>) representing asymmetric stretching of the PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> group. Additional bands (670 cm<sup>−1</sup> and 551 cm<sup>−1</sup>) correspond to P−O symmetric stretching and bending vibrations. Remarkably, the V−O stretching vibration (910 cm<sup>−1</sup>) intensity exceeds V−F vibration (943 cm<sup>−1</sup>), reflecting more V−O bonds in the NVOPF lattice.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0027_R_d18288762e1633" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Raman spectroscopy (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information) exhibits characteristic vibrational modes associated with the PO<sub>4</sub><sup>3−</sup> groups, consistent with the expected molecular structure of NVOPF.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0028_R_d18288762e1644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano Energy">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The sharpness and intensity of the Raman peaks further indicate a well-ordered crystalline structure, aligning with the XRD and FTIR results. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information) presents the elemental signals (C 1s, O 1s, Na 1s, F 1s, P 2p, and V 2p) of the XPS survey spectrum. Notably, the high-resolution XPS spectrum of V 2p in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0005">5d</a> distinctly shows two intensive peaks, indicating V<sup>4+</sup> species, consistent with previous studies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0005_R_d18288762e1658" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Joule">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> UV-vis spectrum of as-prepared NVOPF is compared with those of VCl<sub>3</sub>, VOSO<sub>4</sub>, and NaVO<sub>3</sub>, as presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0005">5e</a>, indicating that the synthesized product presents an absorption band similar to VOSO<sub>4</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0005_R_d18288762e1675" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Joule">5</a>, <a href="#adma202413013-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0029_R_d18288762e1675" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Appl. Catal., B">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> That is, the valence state of vanadium in the synthesized product should be +4, which is in line with the results of Magic-angle spinning solid-state NMR (MAS SSNMR) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0026_R_d18288762e1684" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A">26</a>, <a href="#adma202413013-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0030_R_d18288762e1684" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202413013-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b50bcd08-5758-4cb6-b90a-fcc989486b27/adma202413013-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/b50bcd08-5758-4cb6-b90a-fcc989486b27/adma202413013-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e5a7e497-2ff9-4263-b301-71ee1aec8504/adma202413013-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b50bcd08-5758-4cb6-b90a-fcc989486b27/adma202413013-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202413013-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202413013" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Rietveld-refined XRD profiles, b) 2D GIXRD image. c) The radially integrated intensity profile and d) high-resolution V 2p XPS spectrum of as-synthesized NVOPF. e) UV–vis spectra of as-synthesized product in comparison with VCl<sub>3</sub>, VOSO<sub>4</sub>, and NaVO<sub>3</sub>. f) SEM images of the NVOPF sample at different magnifications. g) TEM image and elemental mapping results of NVOPF. h) HRTEM image and i) SAED pattern of as-synthesized NVOPF.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) characterizes the typical morphology of the as-prepared cathode particles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0005">5f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information), revealing a distinctive “<i>Cuju</i>” ball-like structure with an average particle size of 3&nbsp;µm. This compact, layered architecture maximizes surface area exposure, promoting efficient Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion and faster charge transfer. The spherical arrangement further ensures uniform stress distribution during cycling, thereby reducing mechanical strain and enhancing long-term structural stability. Complementary nitrogen isothermal adsorption-desorption analysis demonstrates a Brunauer-Emmett-Teller (BET) surface area of 12.99 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, featuring a consistent mesoporous distribution at ≈5.8&nbsp;nm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information), which can enhance electrolyte penetration and electrode capacity utilization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0031_R_d18288762e1735" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Transmission electron microscopy (TEM) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information) and energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0005">5g</a>) elemental mapping corroborate SEM observations, confirming morphological characteristics and structural homogeneity. High-resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM) imaging (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0005">5h</a>) reveals an interplanar spacing of 0.53&nbsp;nm within a single particle, matching the (002) plane. The (002) plane possesses a relatively large interlayer spacing, facilitating the insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0032_R_d18288762e1752" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The amorphous thin layer at the material's edge is attributed to high-energy electron beam-induced damage during extended TEM exposure (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0033_R_d18288762e1764" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nature">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0005">5i</a>) further confirms high-crystalline characteristics, with diffraction spots corresponding to (200) and (110) planes along the [001] zone axis, validating the high-quality crystalline nature of NVOPF. These results underscore the effectiveness of our SACFR strategy, facilitated by the DLR mechanism, in achieving superior crystalline quality.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202413013-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202413013-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The Na-storage performance of as-prepared NVOPF cathode is thoroughly evaluated in half-cells. Galvanostatic charge-discharge (GCD) curves (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0006">6a</a>) exhibit two distinct plateaus at ≈3.6 and 4.0&nbsp;V across various current densities (0.1 C to 20 C). Remarkably, at 0.1 C, the cathode exhibits a remarkable reversible discharge capacity of 129.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, approaching the theoretical limit of 130 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Even at a high rate of 20 C, it maintains a discharge capacity of 99.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Upon returning to 0.1 C, it recovers a capacity of up to 128.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, only 1.2% lower than the initial capacity, indicating its exceptional resilience to high-rate cycling (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0006">6b</a>). Further comparison with literature reveals its superior rate performance compared to other recently reported fluorophosphate cathodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0006">6c</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0005_R_d18288762e1802" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Joule">5</a>, <a href="#adma202413013-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0027_R_d18288762e1802" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">27</a>, <a href="#adma202413013-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0034_R_d18288762e1802" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Small InfoMat Sci. Bull. Cell Rep. Phys. Sci.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cycling performance is evaluated at both 1 C and 20 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0006">6d,e</a>). After 1000 cycles at 1 C, NVOPF maintains a capacity of 116.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, exceeding 95% of its initial discharge capacity. Even at 20 C, it demonstrates excellent retention of 80.2% after 5000 cycles, with a capacity loss of only ∼0.004% per cycle, outperforming recent similar-focused studies (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a>, Supporting Information). The nearly overlapping GCD curves at 1 C and the minimal discharge voltage decay at 20 C suggest its high reversibility and stability (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a>, Supporting Information). The long-term cycling life of NVOPF at 5 C and 10 C was also evaluated, with respectable capacity retentions of 84.2% after 3000 cycles and 80.4% after 5000 cycles, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a>, Supporting Information). In terms of preparation cost and Na-storage performance, our low-temperature urea-based synthesis route not only outperforms the high-temperature CA route (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a>, Supporting Information) but also delivers comprehensive properties that are on par with or exceed those of previously reported NVOPF cathodes for SIBs, as summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202413013-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a1778bf1-452e-4035-8c3a-b8314ca62df5/adma202413013-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a1778bf1-452e-4035-8c3a-b8314ca62df5/adma202413013-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d0e75fad-d2d8-4be6-b754-1a7c4caf58d3/adma202413013-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a1778bf1-452e-4035-8c3a-b8314ca62df5/adma202413013-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202413013-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202413013" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) GCD curves and b) rate capability of NVOPF electrode at various rates ranging from 0.1 C to 20 C (1 C = 130&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>). c) Comparison of rate performance. Cycling performance of NVOPF cathode at d) 1 C and e) 20 C. f) Contour plot of CV pattern at different scanning rates. g) Linear fittings of log (<i>i</i><sub>p</sub>) and log (<i>v</i>), and h) GITT plots of NVOPF.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Cyclic voltammetry (CV) and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) are performed to investigate the reaction kinetics contributing to the superior rate performance and excellent cycling stability of NVOPF. The CV pattern at different scanning rates (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0006">6f</a>) reveals two pairs of symmetric redox peaks at 3.65 and 4.05&nbsp;V, indicating excellent reversibility. The <i>b</i>-values for O1, R1, O2, and R2, determined from the formula <i>i</i><sub>p</sub> = <i>av<sup>b</sup></i> (log <i>i</i><sub>p</sub> = <i>b</i> log <i>v</i> + log <i>a</i>), are 0.75, 0.76, 0.78, and 0.77, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0006">6g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting a significant pseudocapacitive contribution to the charge-storage process of the NVOPF cathode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0035_R_d18288762e1890" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Science">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This guarantees the fast-redox kinetics and superior rate performance of NVOPF. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0006">6h</a> shows the GITT plots of NVOPF with negligible overpotential, and the corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup>) are calculated to average ≈1.13 × 10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S33</a>, Supporting Information). These results collectively demonstrate that the as-synthesized NVOPF cathode exhibits remarkable Na diffusion kinetics, characterized by decent pseudocapacitive behavior and high <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> values, further confirming the superiority of products prepared via the DLR mechanism. The exceptional electrochemical performance of NVOPF, particularly its high-rate capabilities and outstanding cycling stability, is rooted in our SACFR strategy based on the DLR mechanism. This approach establishes a dynamic system that intelligently adapts to real-time changes in the coordination environment during synthesis, yielding highly crystalline NVOPF with optimized Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion pathways, thereby enhancing Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, accelerating redox reactions, and ensuring long-term structural stability. The high <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> values and pronounced pseudocapacitive behavior observed are direct indicators of this finely tuned structural optimization. Interestingly, noticeable fluctuations in <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub><sup>+</sup> values are observed at voltage plateaus of 3.6 and 4.0&nbsp;V, which are attributed to the extensive insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> in these voltage regions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0036_R_d18288762e1934" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202413013-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202413013-sec-0060-title">2.4 Structure Evolution</h3>
            
            <p>In situ XRD is conducted to monitor the structural evolution of NVOPF during Na<sup>+</sup> de/intercalation within the voltage range of 2.0−4.3&nbsp;V (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0007">7a,b</a>). During the initial charge process, the (200) peak shifts toward higher angles due to the decrease in the <i>a</i>-lattice parameter induced by vanadium oxidation. Conversely, the repulsive force between pseudolites increases, leading to an expansion in the <i>c</i> value. Subsequently, the (200) peak eventually merges with the (103) peak. Similarly, evident transitions toward higher angles are observed for the (202) peak. After a full cycle, all diffraction peaks return to their original positions without any new phases observed, indicating the high reversibility and highly stable solid-solution reaction mechanism of the NVOPF cathode. The observed low Coulombic efficiency in the charge/discharge curve is primarily attributed to the increased contact resistance and high mass loading used for the free-standing dry-processed electrode configuration in the in situ XRD setup (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S34</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0037_R_d18288762e1963" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nat. Energy">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Lattice parameters are refined and calculated based on the in situ XRD patterns (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0007">7c</a>). Interestingly, the maximum unit cell volume change of NVOPF is merely 2.4%, significantly smaller than those of previously reported NASICON-type cathodes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0007">7d</a>), accounting for the high structural stability and prolonged cycle life of NVOPF. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0007">7e</a> schematically presents the single-phase solid-solution evolutions during the charging-discharging process of the NVOPF. Furthermore, the charge compensation mechanism of NVOPF is systematically elucidated via ex situ XPS, UV–vis spectra, and EPR analyses. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0007">7f</a> displays the V 2p XPS spectra under different electrochemical states. The presence of V<sup>4+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (524.6&nbsp;eV) and V<sup>4+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> (517.3&nbsp;eV) peaks in the pristine electrode indicates the tetravalent state of vanadium. Upon charging to 3.8&nbsp;V, both peaks shift toward higher binding energies. Subsequently, upon reaching a full charge of 4.3&nbsp;V, noticeable shifts of V 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and V 2p<sub>3/2</sub> toward higher binding energies of 525.0 and 517.9&nbsp;eV, respectively, suggest the complete transformation from V<sup>4+</sup> to V<sup>5+</sup>. Notably, during subsequent discharging to 2&nbsp;V, the V 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and V 2p<sub>3/2</sub> peaks return to their original states, demonstrating the high reversibility of valence states. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0007">7g</a> shows the transformation of V<sup>4+</sup> to V<sup>5+</sup> during charging, as evidenced by the disappearance of characteristic peaks at 630 and 765&nbsp;nm in the UV-vis spectra. Conversely, during discharging, the reappearance of these peaks indicates the reduction of V<sup>5+</sup> back to V<sup>4+</sup>, confirming the reversibility of V oxidation states during cycling. Additionally, the high reversibility of valence states is further corroborated by ex situ electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) spectra. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0007">7h</a> presents the continuous-wave (CW) EPR spectra of NVOPF during the charge-discharge process. It is noteworthy that V<sup>4+</sup> can be detected due to the presence of unpaired electrons in its electron orbital, while V<sup>5+</sup> with a 3<i>d</i><sup>0</sup> electron configuration does not generate any detectable EPR signal.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0026_R_d18288762e2025" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon charging to 3.8&nbsp;V, the EPR signal corresponding to V<sup>4+</sup> gradually diminishes, and upon reaching a charge of 4.3&nbsp;V, the EPR signal of the V<sup>4+</sup> state nearly vanishes. Hence, it can be reasonably inferred that V<sup>4+</sup> ions undergo complete oxidation to V<sup>5+</sup> during charging. Conversely, during the discharging process, the EPR signal for V<sup>4+</sup> gradually intensifies, providing further confirmation of the reversibility in the structural evolution of NVOPF. Thus, the detailed structural and electrochemical characterization of NVOPF not only confirms its exceptional performance but also exemplifies the success of the SACFR strategy, positioning it as a groundbreaking approach in the development of high-performance cathode materials.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202413013-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7243e48d-d1ec-4c3c-b82a-932acc140db9/adma202413013-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7243e48d-d1ec-4c3c-b82a-932acc140db9/adma202413013-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b219e5a6-24aa-4754-a38b-958c77af6548/adma202413013-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7243e48d-d1ec-4c3c-b82a-932acc140db9/adma202413013-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202413013-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202413013" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) In situ XRD patterns, and c) Changes in cell parameters during the first cycle of NVOPF. d) Comparison of lattice volume variations (ΔV) between the as-synthesized NVOPF and previously reported NASICON-type cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0003" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0003_R_d18288762e2061" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - ACS Energy Lett. Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv.">3</a>, <a href="#adma202413013-bib-0027" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0027_R_d18288762e2061" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater.">27</a>, <a href="#adma202413013-bib-0031" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0031_R_d18288762e2061" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Adv. Mater.">31</a>, <a href="#adma202413013-bib-0038" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0038_R_d18288762e2061" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nano Energy Nano Energy Small Adv. Energy Mater. ACS Energy Lett. Adv. Mater. Small J. Am. Chem. Soc. Nat. Commun.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> e) Single-phase solid-solution evolutions during cycling of NVOPF. f) Ex situ XPS, g) UV–vis, and h) EPR spectra at various charging–discharging states of NVOPF (whereas 3.8C, 4.3C, 3.8D, and 2D represent the NVOPF cathode charged to 3.8&nbsp;V, charged to 4.3&nbsp;V, discharged to 3.8&nbsp;V, and discharged to 2.0&nbsp;V, respectively).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202413013-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202413013-sec-0070-title">2.5 Air Stability, All-Climate Performance, and Kilogram-Scale Production</h3>
            
            <p>To provide more practical details relevant to its real applications of NVOPF, we further investigate its air stability, all-climate performance, and kilogram-scale production. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0008">8a,b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S35</a> (Supporting Information) show the XRD, XPS, and FTIR comparisons between fresh powder and powder exposed to air for sixteen months. No detectable discrepancies or peak shifts are observed, indicating minimal changes in the material's structure over time. The morphologies remained unchanged (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S36</a>, Supporting Information), with the particle surfaces retaining their well-crystallized structure even after exposure to air for sixteen months (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S37</a>, Supporting Information). These characterizations conducted above ensure almost the same cycling performance for NVOPF stored under different conditions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0008">8c</a>). In addition, all-climate performance was evaluated at both −20 and 50 °C. At −20 °C, NVOPF exhibits a specific capacity of 119.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, surpassing 92% of its performance at 30 °C, thus demonstrating remarkable low-temperature performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0008">8d,e</a>). However, reduced discharge plateaus are observed due to elevated electrolyte viscosity, which could impede ion conductivity and increase internal resistance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202413013-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202413013-bib-0034_R_d18288762e2100" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Small InfoMat Sci. Bull. Cell Rep. Phys. Sci.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Conversely, NVOPF exhibits consistent electrochemical characteristics at 55 °C compared to room temperature, indicating robust performance in hot climates. Furthermore, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0008">8f</a> demonstrates the stable cycling performance of NVOPF, with no capacity decay after 300 cycles at −20 °C and 0.5 C, and 84.8% capacity retention after 1600 cycles at 55 °C and 10 C, highlighting its potential for operation in all climates. The corresponding GCD curves are plotted in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S38</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S39</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202413013-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/be9adaa8-0e2c-415e-934a-ba680b60bf92/adma202413013-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/be9adaa8-0e2c-415e-934a-ba680b60bf92/adma202413013-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/988d53b7-72f3-4aa5-bdfa-d5cbda8507b9/adma202413013-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/be9adaa8-0e2c-415e-934a-ba680b60bf92/adma202413013-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202413013-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202413013" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD, b) XPS, and c) cycling performance comparisons between fresh NVOPF powder and powder exposed to air for sixteen months. d) Discharge curves of NVOPF at different rates at 55, 30, and −20 °C. e) Rate performance and f) cycling performance at 55 and −20 °C. g) Schematic illustrating the working mechanism of the NVOPF//HC full cell. h) Charge and discharge curves of NVOPF cathode and a commercial hard carbon anode. i) Cycling performance of the NVOPF//HC full cell at 1 C. j) Digital photo, k) refined XRD result, and l) cycling performance at 10 C of kg-level NVOPF product.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Moreover, sodium-ion full cells (NVOPF//HC) are assembled to evaluate the versatility of NVOPF, with NVOPF as the cathode and hard carbon (HC) as the anode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0008">8g</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0008">8h</a> shows the GCD curves of the NVOPF cathode and HC anode in the half-cell configuration, along with a preliminary demonstration of powering an LED light. The long-term cycling stability of NVOPF//HC demonstrates 84.5% capacity retention after 100 cycles at 1 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0008">8i</a>), with consistent GCD curves observed throughout (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S40</a>, Supporting Information), underscoring the enduring performance of NVOPF. To evaluate the scalability of our synthesis approach, we design a scalable synthetic experiment involving approximately a 1&nbsp;kg batch of NVOPF product (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0008">8j</a>). The crystal phase (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0008">8k</a>) and morphology (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S41</a>, Supporting Information) of the kilogram-scale NVOPF closely resemble those of the gram-scale product, thus demonstrating exceptional rate performance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S42</a>, Supporting Information) and outstanding long-term cycling stability (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202413013-fig-0008">8l</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S43</a>, Supporting Information). Although scaling up to the kilogram scale often poses challenges in maintaining reaction uniformity and optimizing equipment, these issues can be effectively mitigated by precise control of temperature, mixing, and reaction kinetics. The consistent phase and morphology across scales further demonstrate the superiority of our synthesis strategy. The successful production of kilogram-scale NVOPF confirms the feasibility of this synthesis strategy to provide promising polyanionic cathodes for practical applications.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202413013-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202413013-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, to our knowledge, we are the first to successfully integrate principles from coordination field chemistry, thermodynamics, and reaction kinetics in the low-temperature synthesis of polyanionic cathode materials for SIBs. Our novel SACFR strategy, driven by the DLR mechanism and utilizing urea as a dynamic regulator, effectively manages the coordination field of key reactant ions, resulting in improved reaction kinetics and a reduced <i>ΔG</i>. This makes the reaction thermodynamically and kinetically more favorable at low temperatures, thereby overcoming the momentous challenges of achieving high crystallinity and short reaction times at sub-boiling temperatures, marking a significant improvement over conventional methods. The successful determination of <i>K</i><sub>sp</sub> of NVOPF, combined with extensive calculations (DFT and MD) and comprehensive characterizations (NMR, FTIR, Raman, UV-vis, EPR, and synchrotron-based XAS measurements), further validates our strategy and provides a deeper understanding of the coordination chemistry involved. The as-prepared NVOPF cathodes exhibit exceptional rate performance and ultra-stable cycling stability across a wide temperature range, including room temperature (80.2% capacity retention after 5000 cycles) and extreme conditions (no capacity decay after 300 cycles at −20 °C and 84.8% capacity retention after 1600 cycles at 55 °C), underscoring their potential for all-climate operation. Moreover, the successful kilogram-scale synthesis demonstrates the scalability of the SACFR strategy, paving the way for its industrial implementation. This work not only addresses critical limitations in current synthesis methods but also establishes a new paradigm for the design and development of advanced energy storage materials through dynamic coordination chemistry control.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202413013-sec-0090-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>
